reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Simple model that performs a matrix multiplication, applies Swish activation, and scales the result.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_features, out_features, scaling_factor):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.matmul = nn.Linear(in_features, out_features)
        self.scaling_factor = scaling_factor

    def forward(self, x):
        x = self.matmul(x)
        x = x * torch.sigmoid(x)  # Swish activation
        x = x * self.scaling_factor
        return x

batch_size = 128
in_features = 32768
out_features = 32768
scaling_factor = 2.0

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, in_features)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_features, out_features, scaling_factor]

This task requires writing a custom CUDA kernel to replace a PyTorch model's forward pass that consists of: 1. Linear layer (matmul + bias): y = x @ W^T + b 2. Swish activation: x * sigmoid(x) 3. Scaling: multiply by scaling_factor Given the sizes: batch_size=128, in_features=32768, out_features=32768. This is a large matrix multiplication: 128 x 32768 @ 32768 x 32768. The output is 128 x 32768. First, I should understand the performance characteristics. The matmul dominates. cuBLAS/ cuDNN in PyTorch is heavily optimized for large GEMMs. A pure custom kernel for the matmul likely won't beat cuBLAS unless we do a good job with epilogue fusion. However, we can fuse the bias add, swish activation, and scaling into the matmul kernel. PyTorch might not be fusing all of this by default (though it can with things like addmm and some activation fusions). But given the reference uses explicit operations: x = self.matmul(x) # linear x = x * torch.sigmoid(x) # swish x = x * self.scaling_factor # scale PyTorch likely does `addmm` (fused matmul+bias) then `sigmoid`, then two elementwise multiplies. The memory traffic for these extra passes is large: output size is 128 * 32768 * 4 bytes = 16 MB. That's not huge, but reading/writing it multiple times costs time. Actually, we can make the kernel accept a single tensor and do in-place: ```cuda __global__ void swish_scale_inplace_kernel(float* data, int64_t numel, float scale) { int64_t idx = (int64_t)blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; const int64_t grid_size = (int64_t)blockDim.x * gridDim.x; for (; idx < numel; idx += grid_size) { float x = data[idx]; data[idx] = x / (1.0f + expf(-x)) * scale; } } ``` Wait, I made the same mistake again. `swish(x) = x * sigmoid(x)`, so `data[idx] = x * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x))) * scale;` Let me be very explicit: `swish(x) * scale = x * sigmoid(x) * scaling_factor` `sigmoid(x) = 1 / (1 + exp(-x))` So: `out = x * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-fabsf(x)? no, expf(-x))) * scale` Actually, `expf(-x)` is fine. For large negative x, `expf(-x)` might overflow to inf. But for fp32, `expf(-x)` overflows when `-x > ~88.7`, i.e., `x < -88.7`. In that case, `1.0f / (1.0f + inf) = 0`, which is correct for sigmoid. For large positive x, `expf(-x)` underflows to 0, so `1.0f / (1.0f + 0) = 1`, which is also correct. So `expf(-x)` is numerically safe for sigmoid. However, for better accuracy we might want: `float sig = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x));` or the standard stable form, but for tolerance 1e-4, `expf(-x)` is perfectly fine. Let me think about vectorizing. Let's write a vectorized version: template <typename T> __global__ void swish_scale_inplace_kernel(T* __restrict__ data, int64_t numel, float scale) { int64_t idx = (int64_t)blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int64_t numel_vec = numel / 4; float4* data4 = reinterpret_cast<float4*>(data); for (int64_t i = idx; i < numel_vec; i += gridDim.x * blockDim.x) { float4 v = data4[i]; // process each element v.x = v.x * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-v.x))) * scale; v.y = ... data4[i] = v; } // handle remainder int64_t tail_start = numel_vec * 4; for (int64_t i = tail_start + idx; i < numel; i += gridDim.x * blockDim.x) { float x = data[i]; data[i] = x * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x))) * scale; } } Need to ensure alignment if numel is not multiple of 4. float4 requires 16-byte alignment. But if the data pointer is aligned (it should be from PyTorch CUDA allocations, which are 256-byte aligned), and if numel is multiple of 4, we can do full vectorization. callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "import torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nfrom torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline\n\ncpp_source = r'''\n#include <torch/extension.h>\nvoid swish_scale_inplace_cuda(torch::Tensor data, float scale);\n'''\n\ncuda_source = r'''\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n\n__global__ void swish_scale_inplace_kernel(float* __restrict__ data,\n int64_t numel,\n float scale) {\n int64_t idx = static_cast<int64_t>(blockIdx.x) * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n int64_t stride = static_cast<int64_t>(blockDim.x) * gridDim.x;\n int64_t num_vec = numel / 4;\n float4* data4 = reinterpret_cast<float4*>(data);\n for (int64_t i = idx; i < num_vec; i += stride) {\n float4 v = data4[i];\n v.x = v.x * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-v.x))) * scale;\n v.y = v.y * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-v.y))) * scale;\n v.z = v.z * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-v.z))) * scale;\n v.w = v.w * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-v.w))) * scale;\n data4[i] = v;\n }\n int64_t tail_start = num_vec * 4;\n for (int64_t i = tail_start + idx; i < numel; i += stride) {\n float x = data[i];\n data[i] = x * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x))) * scale;\n }\n}\n\nvoid swish_scale_inplace_cuda(torch::Tensor data, float scale) {\n TORCH_CHECK(data.is_cuda(), \"Tensor must be on CUDA\");\n TORCH_CHECK(data.scalar_type() == at::kFloat, \"Tensor must be float32\");\n int64_t numel = data.numel();\n if (numel == 0) return;\n const int threads = 256;\n const int blocks = static_cast<int>((numel / 4 + threads - 1) / threads);\n swish_scale_inplace_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(data.data_ptr<float>(), numel, scale);\n}\n'''\n\ncuda_module = load_inline(\n name='swish_scale_cuda_vec',\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=['swish_scale_inplace_cuda'],\n extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'],\n verbose=False,\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, in_features, out_features, scaling_factor):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.matmul = nn.Linear(in_features, out_features)\n self.scaling_factor = scaling_factor\n\n def forward(self, x):\n x = self.matmul(x)\n cuda_module.swish_scale_inplace_cuda(x, self.scaling_factor)\n return x\n" }

In standard GEMM with 2D thread blocks, a block handles an M_tile x N_tile output. To compute this, it loads x[M_tile, tile_k] and W[tile_k, N_tile]. As the block iterates over k_tiles, it loads portions of x and W incrementally. Multiple blocks share x through L2 cache when they handle overlapping M_tiles but different N_tiles. In my case, if M=128 and M_tile=128, then no two blocks share the same x rows. But if M_tile < 128, then blocks in the same M-tile row share x through L2. However, if M_tile is small (e.g., 8), then many blocks share x tiles, and L2 can reuse them. For M_tile = 8, N_tile = 32, block size = 256: - Blocks per N for a given M_tile: 32768 / 32 = 1024 - All 1024 blocks in the same M-tile need the same x tiles. Out of 16 M-tiles * 1024 N-tiles = 16384 blocks, we'd process them in ~31 waves. But L2 is ~50MB. x is 16MB. When wave 1 finishes and wave 2 starts, x tiles from wave 1 might still be in L2. So wave 2 can reuse x from L2. This is how cuBLAS achieves good performance. To make this work, we'd want blocks in the same M-tile to be close together in execution, and we'd want enough L2 capacity to hold x tiles across waves. Actually, with 32768 elements and 256 threads: - Phase 1: each thread loads 128 elements, computes local dot product. But loading 128 elements in a strided pattern for W is bad. Alternatively, each block handles one output element (one row, one column). The 256 threads each load and multiply 128 consecutive elements from x and W. But W[:, col] is strided by N=32768. So thread i loads W[i * 128 + offset, col]. This is NOT coalesced. To load W coalesced, the threads in a block need to read W[k, col] for contiguous k. So thread i reads W[i, col] for i in [0, 255], then [256, 511], etc. For 32768 elements, there are 128 iterations. In each iteration, thread i reads W[k + i, col] where k is the base. Since col is fixed, W[k+i, col] for varying i is strided by N*4 = 128KB! NOT coalesced. So a reduction kernel for a single column has terrible memory coalescing for W. This is why GEMMs use tiling instead. OK, so the reduction approach doesn't work well due to memory access patterns. What if I consider transposing the weights? If W is stored as (N, K) = (32768, 32768) instead of (K, N), then W[col, k] for contiguous k is contiguous in memory. For a single column output, reading W[col, :] is a contiguous 32768-element vector. Then a reduction kernel could read this contiguous vector. Wait, PyTorch's nn.Linear stores weights as (out_features, in_features) = (N, K). So W[n, k] for fixed n and varying k IS contiguous! For output element (m, n): output[m, n] = sum_k x[m, k] * W[n, k] So if thread i reads W[n, i] for varying i, and i is contiguous, then W access is coalesced! And x[m, i] for varying i is also coalesced! But if each block computes a single output (m, n), it needs: - x[m, :] which is contiguous (one row of x) - W[n, :] which is contiguous (one row of W) And we want 256 threads to cooperatively reduce over K. Thread i reads x[m, i] and W[n, i] for its portion. But if blocks for different n run concurrently, they all read different rows of W, so there's no coalescing across blocks. Within a block, for output (m, n), thread i in iteration j reads: - x[m, j*256 + i] - W[n, j*256 + i] For contiguous i within a warp, x[m, j*256 + i] reads contiguous memory (good coalescing). W[n, j*256 + i] also reads contiguous memory (good coalescing). So within each block, memory accesses are perfectly coalesced! The issue is that each block only computes one scalar output. For K=32768 and 256 threads, each thread does 128 multiply-adds before the tree reduction. So the compute utilization is reasonable. And since 4,194,304 blocks can run, there's plenty of parallelism. Memory traffic: each block reads M*K + N*K elements of x and W? No. Each block reads one row of x and one row of W. Wait, not even a full row. A block computing output (m, n) only reads x[m, :] and W[n, :]. But multiple blocks computing outputs for the same m but different n will all read the same x[m, :]. There are 32768 such blocks (for n=0 to 32767). So x[m, :] is read 32768 times! Total x traffic = 128 rows * 32768 reads/row * 32768 elements/row * 4 bytes = 5497558138880 bytes = 5.5 TB. Similarly, W[n, :] is read by 128 blocks (for m=0 to 127). So W traffic = 32768 rows * 128 reads/row * 32768 elements * 4 bytes = same = 5.5 TB. At 3.35 TB/s, reading 11 TB takes ~3.3 seconds. That's terrible. But wait! L2 cache should help A LOT here. x is 128 * 32768 * 4 = 16MB. W is 32768 * 32768 * 4 = 4GB. For x: 16MB can fit in L2 cache (~50MB). When the first block for m=0 reads x[0, :], it brings all 16MB (actually 128KB chunks or whatever cache line size) into L2. Subsequent blocks for m=0 also need x[0, :] and find it in L2. So after the first block reads x[0, :], all other blocks for m=0 get x[0, :] from L2. But wait, there are 32768 blocks for m=0. They probably don't all run before blocks for m=1 start. The wave scheduling matters. For W: 4GB doesn't fit in L2. But for a given n, W[n, :] is 128KB (32768 * 4 bytes). L2 is 50MB, so it can hold maybe ~400 W rows. With good scheduling, if all blocks for a given W row run close together, they can reuse W[n, :] from L2. But there are 128 blocks per W row (for each m). If these 128 blocks run as a group, then W[n, :] only needs to be read from DRAM once per group. Total W reads = 32768 rows * 128KB = 4GB. That's exactly the size of W! So with perfect L2 caching (128 blocks per W row running consecutively), W is read exactly once. Similarly for x: each x row is 128KB. L2 can hold ~400 rows. There are 32768 blocks per x row. If they all run consecutively, x row is read once. Total x reads = 128 rows * 128KB = 16MB. So with perfect scheduling, total DRAM traffic = 4GB (W) + 16MB (x) + 2MB (output) = ~4.02GB. But the scheduling is unlikely to be perfect. In practice, with 132 SMs and thousands of blocks in flight, it's likely that blocks for multiple W rows run concurrently, kicking each other out of L2. So W rows get read multiple times. Still, compared to the earlier tiling approach, this might actually work better because: 1. Within a block, memory is perfectly coalesced 2. Each thread does substantial compute (128 MACs per phase, 8 phases with tree reduction overhead) Actually, let me reconsider. In this design, a block computes ONE output element using 256 threads. Each thread reads 128 pairs of (x, W) and accumulates. Then the 256 threads do a parallel reduction (8 steps of shuffle/reduction). Since each block is short, and there are many warps, blocks for the same x row might indeed run close together. This depends on the block scheduling order. If blocks are scheduled in order of (m, n) = (0,0), (0,1), (0,2), ..., (0,32767), (1,0), ... then all 32768 blocks for x row 0 run consecutively. But GPU doesn't necessarily schedule in this order. It schedules based on block coordinates. In a 2D grid launch with grid=(32768, 128), the actual scheduling is typically some variant of round-robin or block-ID based. If the grid is 1D with 4.2M blocks, the order is fixed by blockIdx.x. If I launch a 1D grid of 4.2M blocks where block b computes output (b / 32768, b % 32768), then blocks 0..32767 all compute m=0. For W rows, each W row is needed by blocks with n fixed. Actually, I just realized my kernel uses `1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x))`. PyTorch's sigmoid is actually computed differently on GPU to handle numerical stability for large negative x. For very negative x, exp(-x) overflows in float32 to inf, giving inf / (1 + inf) = 1.0 instead of 0.0. So PyTorch implements sigmoid as: For x >= 0: z = exp(-x); return 1 / (1 + z) For x < 0: z = exp(x); return z / (1 + z) This avoids overflow for large negative x. If my kernel doesn't handle this properly, I might get numerical errors for large negative inputs! The tolerance is 1e-4, but for a random uniform input in [0, 1], x is positive, so exp(-x) is in [e^-1, 1], which doesn't overflow. And after the linear layer, the output could be large positive or negative. But the input to the model is rand([0, 1]), so after linear with random weights, the outputs have mean 0 and some variance. They could be a few standard deviations away from zero. If a weight is large, the output could be very positive or very negative. Wait, the weights are initialized by nn.Linear with the default Kaiming uniform initialization for the default LeCun uniform init. The weights have a bound of `1/sqrt(in_features)` = `1/181`. So for inputs in [0, 1], the outputs are roughly normally distributed with standard deviation around `sqrt(in_features/3) * 1/sqrt(in_features) = 1/sqrt(3) ≈ 0.577` plus bias which is uniform in `[-1/sqrt(in_features), 1/sqrt(in_features)]`. So the linear layer output is typically in [-3, 3] with some outliers. For x = -10, exp(10) = 22026, which doesn't overflow float32 (max ~3.4e38). For x = -100, exp(100) ≈ 2.7e43 which DOES overflow. But outputs of -100 are extremely unlikely given the initialization. Actually, the standard deviation of the output is roughly 0.577 as I calculated. The standard deviation is O(1). So extreme values like -100 are impossibly unlikely. Probably the max |output| is under 10 for most cases. Let me think about this one more time. - x is (128, 32768) = 16MB - W is (32768, 32768) = 4GB If I process outputs in row-major order: - For each row m, compute all N=32768 outputs. - There are 128 rows. - To compute row m, I need x[m, :] which is 128KB. If I use one block per row output (or group of outputs): 1. Load x[m, :] into shared memory? 128KB is too much for SMEM (max 227KB, and we need other things too). 2. So I tile in K. For tile_k=64, load x[m, k:k+64] into SMEM = 256B. Actually, for a group of rows, say 8 rows, x[8, 64] = 2KB. Let's try this: each block computes 8 rows x 64 columns. Block size = 512 threads. - Load x[8, tile_k] into SMEM for each tile_k iteration. - Load W[tile_k, 64] into SMEM. Wait, W[k, n] for k in [tile_k, tile_k+64] and n in [n_start, n_start+64]. This is 64x64 = 4K floats = 16KB. - x[8, 64] = 2KB. Total SMEM = 18KB per block. For 132 SMs, at most ~12 blocks per SM = 1584 concurrent blocks. Total blocks needed: 128/8 * 32768/64 = 16 * 512 = 8192 blocks. 8192 / 1584 ≈ 5 waves. That's reasonable. Memory access pattern: - x tile: x[m:m+8, k:k+64]. For fixed m block and varying k, each x tile is contiguous in memory (row-major). Loading 8 rows x 64 cols = 512 floats. With 512 threads, each thread loads one float. Or with fewer threads, each loads multiple. This is coalesced. - W tile: W[k:k+64, n:n+64]. In (K, N) row-major layout, this is a 64x64 contiguous submatrix. Each row of 64 floats is contiguous. Loading it with 512 threads: each thread loads one float. But W[k+i, n+j] for varying j is contiguous. So threads with contiguous threadIdx will have contiguous n addresses, which is coalesced. Good. Now, each block computes 8*64 = 512 outputs. Each output requires K=32768 MACs, done in 32768/64 = 512 iterations. So each thread computes one output and does 512 MACs. Total FLOPs per thread = 1024 FLOPs. At 67 TFLOPS, this is 15ns per thread. But with memory loads, each iteration loads x(512 floats) + W(4096 floats) = 4608 floats = 18KB. Over 512 iterations: x reads = 512 * 512 * 4 = 1MB. But the x tile for this block is 8*32768 = 256KB! We're reading it 512 times * 2KB = 1MB, which is 4x the x data for this block. That's because we load x in small tiles and reuse them. Wait, for each iteration k, the block loads x[8, k:k+64] which is 2KB. Over 512 iterations, total x loaded = 512 * 2KB = 1MB. But the actual x data needed is x[m:m+8, :] = 8*32768 = 262144 floats = 1MB. So we read exactly 1MB. Good. Similarly for W: each iteration loads W[64, 64] = 16KB. Over 512 iterations, total W loaded = 512 * 16KB = 8MB. But only W[:, n:n+64] is needed, which is 32768 * 64 = 2,097,152 floats = 8MB. So we also read exactly the needed W data. Total memory traffic per block: 1MB (x) + 8MB (W). But x can be reused across blocks in the same M-tile if they run consecutively and if x stays in L2. However, W is different for each block. Total x traffic: there are 16 M-tiles (128/8). Each M-tile needs 1MB of x. Total x = 16MB (read exactly once). Total W traffic: there are 512 N-tiles. Each N-tile needs 8MB of W. Total W = 512 * 8MB = 4096MB = 4GB (read exactly once). Total output traffic: 128 * 32768 * 4 = 16MB (write once). Total DRAM traffic = 16 + 4096 + 16 = 4128MB ≈ 4.03GB. At 3.35TB/s, this takes ~1.2ms just for memory bandwidth. Compute: 2.75e11 FLOPs / 67e12 FLOP/s = 4.1ms. So memory bandwidth takes ~1.2ms and compute takes ~4.1ms. The actual time would be somewhere in between, depending on how well compute and memory overlap. If we can achieve 5ms, this is about the same as cuBLAS. But cuBLAS is extremely well-optimized with microkernels, register blocking, etc. Writing a GEMM this efficient from scratch is very hard. Wait, for H100, global memory is accessed via L2. A warp of 32 threads each doing a float4 load would access 32 * 16 = 512 bytes. This requires 4 cache lines of 128 bytes each. If the base address is aligned to 512 bytes, all threads in the warp access exactly 4 contiguous cache lines, which is fully coalesced. If only aligned to 16 bytes, the warp might span 5 cache lines. PyTorch tensors are usually aligned to large boundaries (e.g., 512 bytes or more). And with M=128, N=32768, the total size is 128*32768*4 = 16,777,216 bytes. The next row starts at index 32768, which is divisible by 4 (float4 index 8192), but is it divisible by... actually the flat array is just one contiguous block. As long as the start is aligned to 16 bytes, and we're processing sequentially, all float4 accesses are aligned. But in a grid-stride loop with blockDim.x * gridDim.x stride, each thread accesses elements at `idx, idx+stride, idx+2*stride, ...`. If `stride` is not a multiple of some warp size * 4, then consecutive threads might not access contiguous memory. Wait! That's a critical issue I overlooked! With grid-stride loop: - Thread 0 accesses elements 0, stride, 2*stride, ... - Thread 1 accesses elements 1, stride+1, 2*stride+1, ... - Thread i accesses elements i, stride+i, 2*stride+i, ... For float4 vectorized access: - Thread 0 loads data4[0], data4[stride/4], ... - Thread 1 loads data4[1], data4[stride/4 + 1], ... If I use a 1D grid, and each thread loads one float4, the threads in a warp load float4 indices [warp_base, warp_base+1, warp_base+2, ..., warp_base+31]. These are contiguous float4 values, so 512 contiguous bytes. This is perfectly coalesced! But then they move to the next iteration: data4[warp_base + stride/4], data4[warp_base + 1 + stride/4], etc. If stride/4 is large, these are far apart. But WITHIN a warp at a given iteration, the accesses are contiguous. And across iterations, the warp accesses a different contiguous chunk. So as long as the grid is 1D and thread indices are contiguous, the float4 accesses within a warp at each step are contiguous. Wait, but with grid-stride loop, all threads in the block access the entire array. If I have 256 threads, in the first iteration they access indices [0, 1, 2, ..., 255] as float4. In the next iteration, they access [stride/4, stride/4+1, ..., stride/4+255] as float4. These are contiguous within each iteration, so coalesced. However, the issue is that after the first iteration, the block moves to a different part of the array. If `stride/4` is large, consecutive iterations access memory that is far apart, which might cause L2 cache misses. But the kernel is launched with a grid that covers the data. If `blocks = numel / 4 / threads`, then stride = blocks * threads = numel / 4. So each thread processes exactly one float4. There's no loop! That's actually better than a grid-stride loop for this case. Each thread has `idx` in [0, 255]. With `num_vec = numel / 4 = stride`, the condition `i < num_vec` means `idx < stride`. callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "import torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nfrom torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline\n\ncpp_source = r'''\n#include <torch/extension.h>\nvoid swish_scale_inplace_cuda(torch::Tensor data, float scale);\n'''\n\ncuda_source = r'''\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n\n__global__ void swish_scale_inplace_kernel(float* __restrict__ data,\n int64_t numel,\n float scale) {\n int64_t idx = static_cast<int64_t>(blockIdx.x) * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n int64_t stride = static_cast<int64_t>(blockDim.x) * gridDim.x;\n int64_t num_vec = numel / 4;\n float4* data4 = reinterpret_cast<float4*>(data);\n #pragma unroll\n for (int64_t i = idx; i < num_vec; i += stride) {\n float4 v = data4[i];\n v.x = v.x * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-v.x))) * scale;\n v.y = v.y * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-v.y))) * scale;\n v.z = v.z * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-v.z))) * scale;\n v.w = v.w * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-v.w))) * scale;\n data4[i] = v;\n }\n int64_t tail_start = num_vec * 4;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int64_t i = tail_start + idx; i < numel; i += stride) {\n float x = data[i];\n data[i] = x * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x))) * scale;\n }\n}\n\nvoid swish_scale_inplace_cuda(torch::Tensor data, float scale) {\n TORCH_CHECK(data.is_cuda(), \"Tensor must be on CUDA\");\n TORCH_CHECK(data.scalar_type() == at::kFloat, \"Tensor must be float32\");\n int64_t numel = data.numel();\n if (numel == 0) return;\n const int threads = 256;\n const int blocks = max(1, static_cast<int>((numel / 4 + threads - 1) / threads));\n swish_scale_inplace_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(data.data_ptr<float>(), numel, scale);\n}\n'''\n\ncuda_module = load_inline(\n name='swish_scale_cuda',\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=['swish_scale_inplace_cuda'],\n extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'],\n verbose=False,\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, in_features, out_features, scaling_factor):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.matmul = nn.Linear(in_features, out_features)\n self.scaling_factor = scaling_factor\n\n def forward(self, x):\n x = self.matmul(x)\n cuda_module.swish_scale_inplace_cuda(x, self.scaling_factor)\n return x\n" }

t < total; t += stride) {\n int i = t / N;\n int j = t % N;\n int64_t offset = static_cast<int64_t>(i) * stride0 + static_cast<int64_t>(j) * stride1;\n float x = data[offset];\n data[offset] = x * (1.0f / (1.0f + __expf(-x))) * scale;\n }\n}\n\nvoid swish_scale_strided_cuda(torch::Tensor data, int stride0, int stride1, int M, int N, float scale) {\n TORCH_CHECK(data.is_cuda(), \"Tensor must be on CUDA\");\n TORCH_CHECK(data.scalar_type() == at::kFloat, \"Tensor must be float32\");\n TORCH_CHECK(M > 0 && N > 0, \"Invalid dimensions\");\n \n const int threads = 256;\n const int blocks = max(1, min(65535, static_cast<int>((static_cast<int64_t>(M) * N + threads - 1) / threads)));\n swish_scale_strided_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(data.data_ptr<float>(), stride0, stride1, M, N, scale);\n}\n'''\n\ncuda_module = load_inline(\n name='swish_scale_strided_cuda',\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=['swish_scale_strided_cuda'],\n extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'],\n verbose=False,\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, in_features, out_features, scaling_factor):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.matmul = nn.Linear(in_features, out_features)\n self.scaling_factor = scaling_factor\n\n def forward(self, x):\n z = torch.addmm(self.matmul.bias.unsqueeze(1), self.matmul.weight, x.t())\n out = z.t()\n cuda_module.swish_scale_strided_cuda(\n out,\n out.stride(0), out.stride(1),\n out.size(0), out.size(1),\n self.scaling_factor\n )\n return out\n" }

Actually, for the GEMM $C = A \times B + D$, where A is [M, K] and B is [K, N], cuBLAS prefers B to be contiguous along K (column-major for B if B^T is used?). Let's analyze. cuBLAS uses column-major by default. But PyTorch tensors are row-major. When you call cuBLAS through PyTorch for `matmul(A, B)`, where A is [M, K] row-major and B is [K, N] row-major, the physical memory is stored as 2D arrays. cuBLAS sees them as column-major transposed if mapped directly. The actual call parameters adjust for this. In `F.linear(x, W)` where W is [N, K]: - PyTorch computes `x @ W.t()`. - `x` is `[B, K]`, `W.t()` is `[K, N]`. - In the GEMM call, `A = x` (M=B, K=K), `B = W.t()` (K=K, N=N). - `W` is stored physically as [N, K] row-major. `W.t()` means we access `W` with strides [1, K] instead of [K, 1]. So `B = W` with a transpose flag. If I instead keep `W_transposed` as [K, N] row-major (contiguous), then in `matmul(x, W_transposed)`, `B` is [K, N] row-major. cuBLAS would see this as a non-transposed B if using row-major semantics, but cuBLAS is column-major. However, PyTorch's dispatcher handles all this. Is there any performance difference depending on whether the weight is transposed in memory? Usually, CUDA GEMM performance is roughly symmetric with respect to transposition because cuBLAS has highly optimized kernels for both. But sometimes there can be a small difference due to better memory access patterns for one configuration. However, for a shape like M=128, K=N=32768, the GEMM is "M-small". In this case, it's essentially a batched matrix-vector product. The performance might be limited by the memory bandwidth of reading the weight matrix. If we compute `x @ W.t()`: - We read `x` (128*32768*4 = 16MB) and `W` (4GB). The output is 16MB. Since W is 4GB, compute is 2*128*32768*32768 = 274 GFLOPs. Arithmetic intensity = 274 / 4 = 68.5 FLOPs/byte (excluding reading x and output). Since H100 ridge point is at ~20 FLOPs/byte, this is compute bound (on FP32, 67 TFLOPS, but on TF32 tensor cores it's much higher). So the matmul is compute-bound if using tensor cores, and memory bandwidth is not the bottleneck. When using TF32 tensor cores on H100 (peak ~494 TFLOPS without sparsity), 274 GFLOPs would take ~0.55ms. If using FP32 (67 TFLOPS), it takes ~4.1ms. The profiled time of ~2.5ms for addmm suggests it might be using tensor cores but not fully saturated, or it's using TF32. Wait, 274 GFLOPs / 2.5ms = 109 TFLOPS. That's in between FP32 and TF32 peak. If cuBLAS is using TF32 tensor cores at ~200 TFLOPS effective, then 274 GFLOPs / 200e12 = 1.37ms. So 2.5ms suggests it's not fully utilizing tensor cores or there's some overhead. For a small M like 128, tensor core utilization might be poor because tensor cores operate on 64x16 or 128x128 tiles. With M=128, it might be decent but not perfect. Still, cuBLAS is the best we have. By storing weight as [K, N] contiguous, we might allow PyTorch to call cuBLAS without the transpose flag on the weight. Does PyTorch call `at::cuda::blas::gemm` with `transpose_b = 't'` or `'n'`? If weight is [N, K] and W.t() is [K, N], then if W is contiguous, W.t() is non-contiguous (strided). cuBLAS supports strided inputs via leading dimension parameters. Is there any penalty for strided access? For W, which is 4GB, if we read it with stride 1 and 32768 (which is the contiguous case for W), that's normal. For W.t(), the stride is 1 and 32768 as well? Wait, W original shape [N, K] row-major, strides [K, 1]. W.t() has shape [K, N], strides [1, K]. This means along the K dimension (rows of W.t.), elements are separated by K floats. When cuBLAS does the GEMM with B = W.t(), it needs to read columns of B (which are rows of W.t.) or rows of B. The leading dimension for W.t. is K. For the GEMM, if B is transposed, then the inner dimension is traversed with stride 1? Actually, in `GEMM(C=alpha*A*B+beta*D)`, A is MxK, B is KxN. In cuBLAS column-major terms, A is MxK lda=M, B is KxN ldb=K. If the physical storage is row-major [N, K], then to represent it as column-major [K, N] with transpose, you set `transb='T'`, and the physical array is [N, K] row-major, which is KxN column-major. The leading dimension of the physical array is K. So cuBLAS handles it naturally. If instead we store the physical array as [K, N] row-major, that's NxK column-major. For the GEMM, we want B to be KxN. In column-major, a KxN matrix is stored with column length K. A physical NxK column-major array with leading dimension N corresponds to a transposed KxN matrix. So we'd set `transb='T'` again to use it as KxN. In both cases, one transpose is needed because of row-major vs column-major mismatch. PyTorch handles this transparently. So I don't think transposing the weight at init yields a significant speedup for the GEMM. Could there be a way to fuse the swish+scale INTO the GEMM kernel, i.e., output the result with swish already applied? This is possible if I write a custom matrix multiplication kernel. However, another simpler idea: Because M=128 is small, we can actually perform the matmul + swish + scale in a single custom kernel by having each block compute one row (or a few rows) of the output. The weight matrix is huge (4GB) and stored in global memory. Each output element requires a dot product of length 32768. - Grid: 128 rows x (32768 output features). - If we assign one thread block per output row, we have 128 blocks. That's too few for the H100's 132 SMs to saturate. - We can assign one block to compute a tile of output features, say 128 or 256 features. Then we'd have 128 * (32768/256) = 16384 blocks, which is good. - Within a block, we can have 256 threads. Each thread computes the dot product for one output feature? No, then each thread does 32768 multiplications and reductions. A reduction across 256 threads is more efficient. A better design for M-small: - Load the input x for one batch element (or a small group) into shared memory. x is 32768 floats = 128KB. Let me now think about how to ensure the custom elementwise kernel is as fast as possible. Using vectorized float4 loads and stores and maximizing occupancy. H100 has SMs that can handle many threads. But the grid size should be calculated properly. For 4.2M elements: - Using float4 (4 elements per thread) gives ~1M vector elements. - With 256 threads per block, we need 4096 blocks. - With 1024 threads per block (max), we need 1024 blocks. - More blocks are usually better for occupancy, as long as each block does enough work. - 4096 blocks of 256 threads = 1,048,576 threads, each processes one vector (4 elements) -> total coverage. Then no tail loop needed (if numel is divisible by 4). numel = 128 * 32768 = 4194304, which is divisible by 4. Great! So no tail loop needed. - But let's also run the tail just in case. Launching 4096 blocks will fully saturate all SMs. Each block has 256 threads = 8 warps. 4096 blocks * 8 warps = 32768 warps. With 132 SMs, that's 248 warps/SM. H100 can support up to 64 warps per SM (2048 threads), so 248 is above capacity, meaning they will be scheduled in waves. Occupancy will be high. Will my vectorized float4 kernel be faster than a scalar kernel? Yes, it halves the number of instructions and improves memory throughput. One more optimization: using `__ldg` for reads if the data is read-only. But we do read+write in-place, so `data` is both read and written. However, we can read with `__ldg` because the previous write to `data[idx]` (by another thread) isn't happening (each thread only touches its own elements). Wait, `__ldg` is for read-only global memory within a kernel or guaranteed no aliasing. Since we do `float x = data[idx];` and then `data[idx] = ...`, and there's no other thread writing to the same index, `__ldg` is safe to use for the load. But then we write to it. The compiler might not let us use `__ldg` for a pointer that we also write to? Actually, `__ldg` just does a load through the read-only cache. It's fine to read via `__ldg` and write via normal store, as long as the address isn't being written by another thread in the same instruction or we don't need the coherency immediately. Since it's the same thread reading and writing, `__ldg` could return stale data if it's cached and we write back without invalidation? No, `__ldg` goes through the read-only constant/texture cache. If the same thread writes back to the same address, it should invalidate the L1 for that address or we could see stale data in a subsequent load. But since we only load once, write once, and don't reload, it's fine. However, the compiler might be conservative and not allow `__ldg` with a generic pointer. A safer option: qualify the pointer as `const float* input` and write to `float* output` if they are the same tensor. But `load_inline` tensor `.data_ptr<float>()` is a mutable pointer. We can cast to `const float*` for `__ldg`. This is a bit tricky but doable. However, since the memory access is perfectly coalesced and we use float4, the standard load/store instructions should already saturate memory bandwidth. The real bottleneck for the elementwise kernel is either memory bandwidth or SFU throughput. On H100, the SFU (special function unit) throughput for `expf` is quite high. Let's see if `expf` is the bottleneck. Actually, A100 had 16 special function units per SM. Not sure about H100. But even if it's 4 `expf` per thread for 1M threads, each thread does one vector load, 4 expf, some FMAs, one vector store. Let me finalize the Python module: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> void swish_scale_inplace_cuda(torch::Tensor data, float scale); ''' cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> __global__ void swish_scale_inplace_kernel(float* __restrict__ data, int64_t numel, float scale) { int64_t idx = static_cast<int64_t>(blockIdx.x) * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; swish_scale_inplace_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(data.data_ptr<float>(), numel, scale); } ''' cuda_module = load_inline( name='swish_scale_cuda', cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['swish_scale_inplace_cuda'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_features, out_features, scaling_factor): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.matmul = nn.Linear(in_features, out_features) self.scaling_factor = scaling_factor def forward(self, x): x = self.matmul(x) cuda_module.swish_scale_inplace_cuda(x, self.scaling_factor) return x ``` I think this is the best I can do. reasoning
I already know the vectorized version has perfect numerical match and should be fast enough — it was already passing correctness and static check. The main remaining question is whether the fused Swish+scale kernel gives enough speedup on top of the dominant `linear`. Since the linear itself takes ~82 ms, saving ~10 μs from Swish is a very small fraction, but it’s the only operation I can realistically optimize here. However, I can think of one more optimization: combine addmm + Swish + scale into a single custom kernel using CUTLASS or manual algebra, but that would be a much larger undertaking and I only have limited iterations. Since the vectorized fused Swish+Scale kernel already matches perfectly, I should just submit that as the final answer. But wait — there may be still room to speed up the linear itself. PyTorch `nn.Linear` already uses highly optimized cuBLAS matmul, but there might be a slight overhead from `nn.Linear` vs manually calling `torch.addmm`. The reference does `self.matmul(x)` which translates internally to `x @ weight.T + bias`. There are a couple of equivalent forms: - `torch.nn.functional.linear(x, self.weight, self.bias)` equivalent. - `torch.addmm(self.bias, x, self.weight.t())` if `x` is 2D. - `torch.matmul(x, self.weight.t()) + self.bias`. All of these go through the same optimized cuBLAS path. Could there be a slight overhead from `self.matmul(x)` because it has to call `F.linear`, create views, etc.? Let's check by rewriting `forward` to use `torch.addmm` directly, with an explicit bias unsqueeze and matrix product. This is essentially what PyTorch does internally but maybe with less Python overhead. Actually, wait — `nn.Linear` calls `F.linear` which does `input.matmul(weight.t()) + bias`. For 2D input this calls `addmm`. The overhead of `F.linear` wrapper is negligible compared to the matmul operation. But maybe there is a faster path: using `torch.mm` and then add bias and apply Swish in the same kernel. That could save one memory pass over the output. We could use the same vectorized float4 kernel but process the output of `torch.mm` with bias addition fused in. That saves writing the output back to L2/DRAM after matmul and then reading it back. The saved time would be the elapsed of `torch.sigmoid` (~10.5 μs) but also reduce the latency of the `linear` part by maybe 2–3 μs because `addmm` may be fused into `mm`+fused bias+Swish. But `torch.mm` + custom bias+Swish would still need to read the weight matrix, which cuBLAS handles. cuBLAS `cublasGemmEx` with FP32 takes ~82 ms; adding bias+Swish afterward is a small operation on large data. If I want more speedup, I might try to call `torch.mm` instead of `torch.addmm` (to avoid any potential `beta*C` data movement overhead when `size(C)=size(output)`), then my fused kernel adds bias and Swish+scale. Actually, the bias addition is elementwise and easy to fuse. The total reads would be: matmul output (from matmul engine) written to memory, then our kernel reads it, adds bias, applies Swish+scale, writes back. Wait, that seems what `addmm` does too — it computes `alpha * A * B + beta * C`, with `C` being the pre-allocated bias-broadcasted output buffer. If `beta=0` and then the bias is broadcast-added, `addmm` may internally fuse everything, or not. If our kernel fuses bias addition with Swish+scale, we eliminate the `bias + (x @ W.T)` overhead and potentially that kernel call. But the `matmul` kernel still writes output to memory, and our kernel reads and writes it — which is unavoidable if we can't modify the cuBLAS call itself. So the practical speedup is mainly from fusing the elementwise operations, which will give a small but measurable improvement. Let's just submit the vectorized fused version. Before submitting, I'll re-check correctness one more time for the version with explicit vectorized float4 and `--use_fast_math`. `expf` is the same function call with or without `--use_fast_math`; `--use_fast_math` might turn it into `__expf` intrinsic. Our kernel passed correctness with `max_abs_err=0.00e+00` — maybe because the compiler didn't actually replace it? import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

cpp_source = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>
void swish_scale_inplace_cuda(torch::Tensor data, float scale);
'''

cuda_source = r'''
#include <torch/extension.h>

__global__ void swish_scale_inplace_kernel(float* __restrict__ data,
                                            int64_t numel,
                                            float scale) {
    int64_t idx = static_cast<int64_t>(blockIdx.x) * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;
    int64_t stride = static_cast<int64_t>(blockDim.x) * gridDim.x;
    int64_t num_vec = numel >> 2;
    float4* data4 = reinterpret_cast<float4*>(data);
    #pragma unroll
    for (int64_t i = idx; i < num_vec; i += stride) {
        float4 v = data4[i];
        v.x = v.x * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-v.x))) * scale;
        v.y = v.y * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-v.y))) * scale;
        v.z = v.z * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-v.z))) * scale;
        v.w = v.w * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-v.w))) * scale;
        data4[i] = v;
    }
    int64_t tail_start = num_vec << 2;
    #pragma unroll
    for (int64_t i = tail_start + idx; i < numel; i += stride) {
        float x = data[i];
        data[i] = x * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x))) * scale;
    }
}

void swish_scale_inplace_cuda(torch::Tensor data, float scale) {
    TORCH_CHECK(data.is_cuda(), "Tensor must be on CUDA");
    TORCH_CHECK(data.scalar_type() == at::kFloat, "Tensor must be float32");
    int64_t numel = data.numel();
    if (numel == 0) return;
    const int threads = 256;
    int blocks = (numel >= 4)
        ? static_cast<int>((numel / 4 + threads - 1) / threads)
        : 1;
    if (blocks < 1) blocks = 1;
    swish_scale_inplace_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(data.data_ptr<float>(), numel, scale);
}
'''

cuda_module = load_inline(
    name="swish_scale_cuda_x",
    cpp_sources=cpp_source,
    cuda_sources=cuda_source,
    functions=["swish_scale_inplace_cuda"],
    extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"],
    verbose=False,
)

class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, in_features, out_features, scaling_factor):
        super(ModelNew, self).__init__()
        self.matmul = nn.Linear(in_features, out_features)
        self.scaling_factor = scaling_factor

    def forward(self, x):
        x = self.matmul(x)
        cuda_module.swish_scale_inplace_cuda(x, self.scaling_factor)
        return x
